<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Ecology</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Study of organisms and their environment</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Ethology" title="Ethology">ethology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethnology" title="Ethnology">ethnology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Etiology" title="Etiology">etiology</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Deep_ecology" title="Deep ecology">deep ecology</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Ecology_(disambiguation)" title="Ecology (disambiguation)">Ecology (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

addresses the full scale of life, from tiny bacteria to processes that span the entire planet. Ecologists study many diverse and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interspecific_interactions" title="Interspecific interactions">complex relations</a> among species, such as <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">predation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pollination" title="Pollination">pollination</a>. The diversity of life is organized into different <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habitats" title="Habitats">habitats</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_ecosystem" title="Terrestrial ecosystem">terrestrial</a> (middle) to <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_ecosystem" title="Aquatic ecosystem">aquatic ecosystems</a>.</td></tr></tbody></table>

.navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:TopicTOC-Biology" title="Template:TopicTOC-Biology"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:TopicTOC-Biology" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:TopicTOC-Biology&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Ecology</b> (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a> <i> </i><span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%BF%E1%BC%B6%CE%BA%CE%BF%CF%82#Ancient_Greek" title="wikt:οἶκος">οἶκος</a></span><i> (<span lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek transliteration"><i>oîkos</i></span>)</i> 'house', and <i> </i><span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/-%CE%BB%CE%BF%CE%B3%CE%AF%CE%B1#Ancient_Greek" title="wikt:-λογία">-λογία</a></span><i> (<span lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek transliteration"><i><a href="/wiki/-logy" title="-logy">-logía</a></i></span>)</i> 'study of')<sup class="reference" id="ref_A_1"><a href="#cnote_A">[A]</a></sup> is the study of the relationships between living <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">humans</a>, and their <a href="/wiki/Biophysical_environment" title="Biophysical environment">physical environment</a>. Ecology considers organisms at the individual, <a href="/wiki/Population" title="Population">population</a>, <a href="/wiki/Community_(ecology)" title="Community (ecology)">community</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a> level. Ecology overlaps with the closely related sciences of <a href="/wiki/Biogeography" title="Biogeography">biogeography</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_biology" title="Evolutionary biology">evolutionary biology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethology" title="Ethology">ethology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Natural_history" title="Natural history">natural history</a>. Ecology is a branch of <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, and it is not synonymous with <a href="/wiki/Environmentalism" title="Environmentalism">environmentalism</a>.
</p><p>Among other things, ecology is the study of:
</p>
<ul><li>Life processes, <a href="/wiki/Antifragility" title="Antifragility">antifragility</a>, interactions, and <a href="/wiki/Adaptation" title="Adaptation">adaptations</a></li>
<li>The movement of materials and <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> through living communities</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Ecological_succession" title="Ecological succession">successional</a> development of ecosystems</li>
<li>Cooperation, competition, and predation within and between <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Abundance_(ecology)" title="Abundance (ecology)">abundance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biomass_(ecology)" title="Biomass (ecology)">biomass</a>, and distribution of organisms in the context of the environment</li>
<li>Patterns of <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a> and its effect on ecosystem processes</li></ul>
<p>Ecology has practical applications in <a href="/wiki/Conservation_biology" title="Conservation biology">conservation biology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">wetland</a> management, <a href="/wiki/Natural_resource_management" title="Natural resource management">natural resource management</a> (<a href="/wiki/Agroecology" title="Agroecology">agroecology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Forestry" title="Forestry">forestry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agroforestry" title="Agroforestry">agroforestry</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fisheries" title="Fisheries">fisheries</a>), <a href="/wiki/Urban_planning" title="Urban planning">urban planning</a> (<a href="/wiki/Urban_ecology" title="Urban ecology">urban ecology</a>), <a href="/wiki/Community_health" title="Community health">community health</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ecological_economics" title="Ecological economics">economics</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basic_science" title="Basic science">basic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Applied_science" title="Applied science">applied science</a>, and human social interaction (<a href="/wiki/Human_ecology" title="Human ecology">human ecology</a>).
</p><p>The word <i>ecology</i> (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <i lang="de">Ökologie</i>) was coined in 1866 by the German scientist <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Haeckel" title="Ernst Haeckel">Ernst Haeckel</a>, and it became a rigorous <a href="/wiki/Natural_science" title="Natural science">science</a> in the late 19th century. <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">Evolutionary</a> concepts relating to adaptation and <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a> are cornerstones of modern <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_ecology" title="Theoretical ecology">ecological theory</a>.
</p><p>Ecosystems are dynamically interacting systems of organisms, the communities they make up, and the non-living (<a href="/wiki/Abiotic_component" title="Abiotic component">abiotic</a>) components of their environment. Ecosystem processes, such as <a href="/wiki/Primary_production" title="Primary production">primary production</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nutrient_cycling" title="Nutrient cycling">nutrient cycling</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Niche_construction" title="Niche construction">niche construction</a>, regulate the flux of energy and matter through an environment. Ecosystems have <a href="/wiki/Biophysics" title="Biophysics">biophysical</a> feedback mechanisms that moderate processes acting on living (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biotic_component" title="Biotic component">biotic</a>) and abiotic components of the planet. Ecosystems sustain life-supporting functions and provide <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_service" title="Ecosystem service">ecosystem services</a> like <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> production (food, fuel, fiber, and medicine), the regulation of <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a>, global <a href="/wiki/Biogeochemical_cycle" title="Biogeochemical cycle">biogeochemical cycles</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_filtration" title="Water filtration">water filtration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soil_formation" title="Soil formation">soil formation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">erosion</a> control, flood protection, and many other natural features of scientific, historical, economic, or intrinsic value.
</p>

<h2><span id="Levels.2C_scope.2C_and_scale_of_organization"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Levels,_scope,_and_scale_of_organization">Levels, scope, and scale of organization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ecology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Levels, scope, and scale of organization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Outline_of_ecology" title="Outline of ecology">Outline of ecology</a></div>
<p>The scope of ecology contains a wide array of interacting levels of organization spanning micro-level (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a>) to a planetary scale (e.g., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_spheres" title="Earth's spheres">biosphere</a>) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phenomena" title="Phenomena">phenomena</a>. Ecosystems, for example, contain abiotic <a href="/wiki/Resource_(biology)" title="Resource (biology)">resources</a> and interacting life forms (i.e., individual organisms that aggregate into <a href="/wiki/Population" title="Population">populations</a> which aggregate into distinct ecological communities). Ecosystems are dynamic, they do not always follow a linear successional path, but they are always changing, sometimes rapidly and sometimes so slowly that it can take thousands of years for ecological processes to bring about certain <a href="/wiki/Ecological_succession" title="Ecological succession">successional stages</a> of a forest. An ecosystem's area can vary greatly, from tiny to vast. A single tree is of little consequence to the classification of a forest ecosystem, but critically relevant to organisms living in and on it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stadler98_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stadler98-1">[1]</a></sup> Several generations of an <a href="/wiki/Aphid" title="Aphid">aphid</a> population can exist over the lifespan of a single leaf. Each of those aphids, in turn, support diverse <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacterial</a> communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Humphreys97_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Humphreys97-2">[2]</a></sup> The nature of connections in ecological communities cannot be explained by knowing the details of each species in isolation, because the emergent pattern is neither revealed nor predicted until the ecosystem is studied as an integrated whole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Liere2012_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Liere2012-3">[3]</a></sup> Some ecological principles, however, do exhibit collective properties where the sum of the components explain the properties of the whole, such as birth rates of a population being equal to the sum of individual births over a designated time frame.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Odum05_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Odum05-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>The main subdisciplines of ecology, <a href="/wiki/Population_ecology" title="Population ecology">population</a> (or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Community_ecology" title="Community ecology">community</a>) ecology and <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_ecology" title="Ecosystem ecology">ecosystem ecology</a>, exhibit a difference not only of scale but also of two contrasting paradigms in the field. The former focuses on organisms' distribution and abundance, while the latter focuses on materials and energy fluxes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hierarchy">Hierarchy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ecology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Hierarchy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Biological_organisation" title="Biological organisation">Biological organisation</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_classification" title="Biological classification">Biological classification</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25%; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>System behaviors must first be arrayed into different levels of organization. Behaviors corresponding to higher levels occur at slow rates. Conversely, lower organizational levels exhibit rapid rates. For example, individual tree leaves respond rapidly to momentary changes in light intensity, CO<sub>2</sub> concentration, and the like. The growth of the tree responds more slowly and integrates these short-term changes.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">O'Neill et al. (1986)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O'Neill86_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-O'Neill86-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 76">: 76 </span></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>The scale of ecological dynamics can operate like a closed system, such as aphids migrating on a single tree, while at the same time remain open with regard to broader scale influences, such as atmosphere or climate. Hence, ecologists classify <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecosystems" title="Ecosystems">ecosystems</a> hierarchically by analyzing data collected from finer scale units, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vegetation_association" title="Vegetation association">vegetation associations</a>, climate, and <a href="/wiki/Soil_type" title="Soil type">soil types</a>, and integrate this information to identify emergent patterns of uniform organization and processes that operate on local to regional, <a href="/wiki/Landscape" title="Landscape">landscape</a>, and chronological scales.
</p><p>To structure the study of ecology into a conceptually manageable framework, the biological world is organized into a <a href="/wiki/Biological_organisation" title="Biological organisation">nested hierarchy</a>, ranging in scale from <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Tissue_(biology)" title="Tissue (biology)">tissues</a>, to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organ_(anatomy)" title="Organ (anatomy)">organs</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Population_ecology" title="Population ecology">populations</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Community_(ecology)" title="Community (ecology)">communities</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Biome" title="Biome">biomes</a>, and up to the level of the <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nachtomy01_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nachtomy01-7">[7]</a></sup> This framework forms a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panarchy_(ecology)" title="Panarchy (ecology)">panarchy</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holling01_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holling01-8">[8]</a></sup> and exhibits <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-linear" title="Non-linear">non-linear</a> behaviors; this means that "effect and cause are disproportionate, so that small changes to critical variables, such as the number of <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_fixation" title="Nitrogen fixation">nitrogen fixers</a>, can lead to disproportionate, perhaps irreversible, changes in the system properties."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levin99_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levin99-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 14">: 14 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiversity">Biodiversity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ecology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Biodiversity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">Biodiversity</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1062260506" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25%; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>Biodiversity refers to the variety of life and its processes. It includes the variety of living organisms, the genetic differences among them, the communities and ecosystems in which they occur, and the ecological and <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolutionary</a> processes that keep them functioning, yet ever changing and adapting.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">Noss &amp; Carpenter (1994)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Noss94_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Noss94-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 5">: 5 </span></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>Biodiversity (an abbreviation of "biological diversity") describes the diversity of life from genes to ecosystems and spans every level of biological organization. The term has several interpretations, and there are many ways to index, measure, characterize, and represent its complex organization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Noss90_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Noss90-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scholes08_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scholes08-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cardinale2012_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-cardinale2012-13">[13]</a></sup> Biodiversity includes <a href="/wiki/Species_diversity" title="Species diversity">species diversity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_diversity" title="Ecosystem diversity">ecosystem diversity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Genetic_diversity" title="Genetic diversity">genetic diversity</a> and scientists are interested in the way that this diversity affects the complex ecological processes operating at and among these respective levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scholes08_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Scholes08-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilson00b_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson00b-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Purvis00_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Purvis00-15">[15]</a></sup> Biodiversity plays an important role in <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_service" title="Ecosystem service">ecosystem services</a> which by definition maintain and improve human quality of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cardinale2012_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-cardinale2012-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ostfeld09_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ostfeld09-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tierney09_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tierney09-17">[17]</a></sup> Conservation priorities and management techniques require different approaches and considerations to address the full ecological scope of biodiversity. <a href="/wiki/Natural_capital" title="Natural capital">Natural capital</a> that supports populations is critical for maintaining <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecosystem_services" title="Ecosystem services">ecosystem services</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ceballos02_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ceballos02-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Palumbi09_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Palumbi09-19">[19]</a></sup> and species <a href="/wiki/Animal_migration" title="Animal migration">migration</a> (e.g., riverine fish runs and avian insect control) has been implicated as one mechanism by which those service losses are experienced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilcove08_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilcove08-20">[20]</a></sup> An understanding of biodiversity has practical applications for species and ecosystem-level conservation planners as they make management recommendations to consulting firms, governments, and industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hammond09_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hammond09-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Habitat">Habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ecology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">Habitat</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG/170px-Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG/255px-Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG/340px-Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blue_Linckia_Starfish.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Biodiversity of a <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">coral reef</a>. <a href="/wiki/Coral" title="Coral">Corals</a> adapt to and modify their environment by forming <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">calcium carbonate</a> skeletons. This provides growing conditions for future generations and forms a habitat for many other species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kiessling09_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kiessling09-22">[22]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Long-tailed_Broadbill_(Psarisomus_dalhousiae).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2498" data-file-width="3752" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Long-tailed_Broadbill_%28Psarisomus_dalhousiae%29.jpg/220px-Long-tailed_Broadbill_%28Psarisomus_dalhousiae%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Long-tailed_Broadbill_%28Psarisomus_dalhousiae%29.jpg/330px-Long-tailed_Broadbill_%28Psarisomus_dalhousiae%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Long-tailed_Broadbill_%28Psarisomus_dalhousiae%29.jpg/440px-Long-tailed_Broadbill_%28Psarisomus_dalhousiae%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Long-tailed_Broadbill_(Psarisomus_dalhousiae).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Long-tailed_broadbill" title="Long-tailed broadbill">Long-tailed broadbill</a> building its <a href="/wiki/Bird_nest" title="Bird nest">nest</a></div></div></div>
<p>The habitat of a species describes the environment over which a species is known to occur and the type of community that is formed as a result.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whittaker73_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whittaker73-23">[23]</a></sup> More specifically, "habitats can be defined as regions in environmental space that are composed of multiple dimensions, each representing a biotic or abiotic environmental variable; that is, any component or characteristic of the environment related directly (e.g. forage biomass and quality) or indirectly (e.g. elevation) to the use of a location by the animal."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beyer10_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beyer10-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 745">: 745 </span></sup> For example, a habitat might be an aquatic or terrestrial environment that can be further categorized as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Montane_ecosystem" title="Montane ecosystem">montane</a> or <a href="/wiki/Alpine_tundra" title="Alpine tundra">alpine</a> ecosystem. Habitat shifts provide important evidence of competition in nature where one population changes relative to the habitats that most other individuals of the species occupy. For example, one population of a species of tropical lizard (<i>Tropidurus hispidus</i>) has a flattened body relative to the main populations that live in open savanna. The population that lives in an isolated rock outcrop hides in crevasses where its flattened body offers a selective advantage. Habitat shifts also occur in the developmental <a href="/wiki/Life_history_theory" title="Life history theory">life history</a> of amphibians, and in insects that transition from aquatic to terrestrial habitats. <a href="/wiki/Biotope" title="Biotope">Biotope</a> and habitat are sometimes used interchangeably, but the former applies to a community's environment, whereas the latter applies to a species' environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whittaker73_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Whittaker73-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schoener75_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schoener75-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vitt97_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vitt97-26">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Niche">Niche</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ecology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Niche">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">Ecological niche</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Termite_mound-Tanzania.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1203" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Termite_mound-Tanzania.jpg/170px-Termite_mound-Tanzania.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Termite_mound-Tanzania.jpg/255px-Termite_mound-Tanzania.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Termite_mound-Tanzania.jpg/340px-Termite_mound-Tanzania.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Termite_mound-Tanzania.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Termite" title="Termite">Termite</a> mounds with varied heights of chimneys regulate gas exchange, temperature and other environmental parameters that are needed to sustain the internal physiology of the entire colony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laland99_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Laland99-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes08_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes08-28">[28]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Definitions of the niche date back to 1917,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wiens05_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wiens05-29">[29]</a></sup> but <a href="/wiki/G._Evelyn_Hutchinson" title="G. Evelyn Hutchinson">G. Evelyn Hutchinson</a> made conceptual advances in 1957<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hutchinson57_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hutchinson57-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hutchinson57b_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hutchinson57b-31">[31]</a></sup> by introducing a widely adopted definition:  "the set of biotic and abiotic conditions in which a species is able to persist and maintain stable population sizes."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wiens05_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wiens05-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 519">: 519 </span></sup> The ecological niche is a central concept in the ecology of organisms and is sub-divided into the <i>fundamental</i> and the <i>realized</i> niche. The fundamental niche is the set of environmental conditions under which a species is able to persist. The realized niche is the set of environmental plus ecological conditions under which a species persists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wiens05_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wiens05-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hutchinson57b_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hutchinson57b-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Begon05_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Begon05-32">[32]</a></sup> The Hutchinsonian niche is defined more technically as a "<a href="/wiki/Euclidean_space" title="Euclidean space">Euclidean</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N-dimensional_space" title="N-dimensional space">hyperspace</a> whose <i>dimensions</i> are defined as environmental variables and whose <i>size</i> is a function of the number of values that the environmental values may assume for which an organism has <i>positive fitness</i>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hardesty75_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hardesty75-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 71">: 71 </span></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Biogeography" title="Biogeography">Biogeographical</a> patterns and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Range_(biology)" title="Range (biology)">range</a> distributions are explained or predicted through knowledge of a species' <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trait_(biology)" title="Trait (biology)">traits</a> and niche requirements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pearman08_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pearman08-34">[34]</a></sup> Species have functional traits that are uniquely adapted to the ecological niche. A trait is a measurable property, <a href="/wiki/Phenotype" title="Phenotype">phenotype</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Phenotypic_trait" title="Phenotypic trait">characteristic</a> of an organism that may influence its survival. Genes play an important role in the interplay of development and environmental expression of traits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levins80_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levins80-35">[35]</a></sup> Resident species evolve traits that are fitted to the selection pressures of their local environment. This tends to afford them a competitive advantage and discourages similarly adapted species from having an overlapping geographic range. The <a href="/wiki/Competitive_exclusion_principle" title="Competitive exclusion principle">competitive exclusion principle</a> states that two species cannot coexist indefinitely by living off the same limiting <a href="/wiki/Resource_(biology)" title="Resource (biology)">resource</a>; one will always out-compete the other. When similarly adapted species overlap geographically, closer inspection reveals subtle ecological differences in their habitat or dietary requirements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hardin60_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hardin60-36">[36]</a></sup> Some models and empirical studies, however, suggest that disturbances can stabilize the co-evolution and shared niche occupancy of similar species inhabiting species-rich communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scheffer06_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scheffer06-37">[37]</a></sup> The habitat plus the niche is called the <a href="/wiki/Ecotope" title="Ecotope">ecotope</a>, which is defined as the full range of environmental and biological variables affecting an entire species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whittaker73_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-Whittaker73-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Niche_construction">Niche construction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ecology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Niche construction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Niche_construction" title="Niche construction">Niche construction</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecosystem_engineering" title="Ecosystem engineering">Ecosystem engineering</a></div>
<p>Organisms are subject to environmental pressures, but they also modify their habitats. The <a href="/wiki/Negative_feedback" title="Negative feedback">regulatory feedback</a> between organisms and their environment can affect conditions from local (e.g., a <a href="/wiki/Beaver" title="Beaver">beaver</a> <a href="/wiki/Pond" title="Pond">pond</a>) to global scales, over time and even after death, such as decaying logs or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silica" title="Silica">silica</a> skeleton deposits from marine organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hastings07_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hastings07-38">[38]</a></sup> The process and concept of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecosystem_engineering" title="Ecosystem engineering">ecosystem engineering</a> are related to <a href="/wiki/Niche_construction" title="Niche construction">niche construction</a>, but the former relates only to the physical modifications of the habitat whereas the latter also considers the evolutionary implications of physical changes to the environment and the feedback this causes on the process of natural selection. Ecosystem engineers are defined as: "organisms that directly or indirectly modulate the availability of resources to other species, by causing physical state changes in biotic or abiotic materials. In so doing they modify, maintain and create habitats."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones94_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jones94-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 373">: 373 </span></sup>
</p><p>The ecosystem engineering concept has stimulated a new appreciation for the influence that organisms have on the ecosystem and evolutionary process. The term "niche construction" is more often used in reference to the under-appreciated feedback mechanisms of natural selection imparting forces on the abiotic niche.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laland99_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Laland99-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Write06_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Write06-40">[40]</a></sup> An example of natural selection through ecosystem engineering occurs in the nests of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_insects" title="Social insects">social insects</a>, including ants, bees, wasps, and termites. There is an emergent <a href="/wiki/Homeostasis" title="Homeostasis">homeostasis</a> or <a href="/wiki/Homeorhesis" title="Homeorhesis">homeorhesis</a> in the structure of the nest that regulates, maintains and defends the physiology of the entire colony. Termite mounds, for example, maintain a constant internal temperature through the design of air-conditioning chimneys. The structure of the nests themselves is subject to the forces of natural selection. Moreover, a nest can survive over successive generations, so that progeny inherit both genetic material and a legacy niche that was constructed before their time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Odum05_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Odum05-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laland99_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-Laland99-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hughes08_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes08-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biome">Biome</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ecology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Biome">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Biome" title="Biome">Biome</a></div>
<p>Biomes are larger units of organization that categorize regions of the Earth's ecosystems, mainly according to the structure and composition of vegetation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Palmer94_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Palmer94-41">[41]</a></sup> There are different methods to define the continental boundaries of biomes dominated by different functional types of vegetative communities that are limited in distribution by climate, precipitation, weather and other environmental variables. Biomes include <a href="/wiki/Tropical_rainforest" title="Tropical rainforest">tropical rainforest</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperate_broadleaf_and_mixed_forest" title="Temperate broadleaf and mixed forest">temperate broadleaf and mixed forest</a>, <a href="/wiki/Temperate_deciduous_forest" title="Temperate deciduous forest">temperate deciduous forest</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taiga" title="Taiga">taiga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">tundra</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hot_desert" title="Hot desert">hot desert</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Polar_desert" title="Polar desert">polar desert</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prentice92_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prentice92-42">[42]</a></sup> Other researchers have recently categorized other biomes, such as the human and oceanic <a href="/wiki/Microbiome" title="Microbiome">microbiomes</a>. To a <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microbe</a>, the human body is a habitat and a landscape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turnbaugh07_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Turnbaugh07-43">[43]</a></sup> Microbiomes were discovered largely through advances in <a href="/wiki/Molecular_genetics" title="Molecular genetics">molecular genetics</a>, which have revealed a hidden richness of microbial diversity on the planet. The oceanic microbiome plays a significant role in the ecological biogeochemistry of the planet's oceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeLong09_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-DeLong09-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biosphere">Biosphere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ecology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Biosphere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">Biosphere</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_spheres" title="Earth's spheres">Earth's spheres</a></div>
<p>The largest scale of ecological organization is the biosphere: the total sum of ecosystems on the planet. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecological_relationship" title="Ecological relationship">Ecological relationships</a> regulate the flux of energy, nutrients, and climate all the way up to the planetary scale. For example, the dynamic history of the planetary atmosphere's CO<sub>2</sub> and O<sub>2</sub> composition has been affected by the biogenic flux of gases coming from respiration and photosynthesis, with levels fluctuating over time in relation to the ecology and evolution of plants and animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-igamberdiev06_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-igamberdiev06-45">[45]</a></sup> Ecological theory has also been used to explain self-emergent regulatory phenomena at the planetary scale: for example, the <a href="/wiki/Gaia_hypothesis" title="Gaia hypothesis">Gaia hypothesis</a> is an example of <a href="/wiki/Holism" title="Holism">holism</a> applied in ecological theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lovelock73_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lovelock73-46">[46]</a></sup> The Gaia hypothesis states that there is an emergent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feedback_loop" title="Feedback loop">feedback loop</a> generated by the <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_theory_of_ecology" title="Metabolic theory of ecology">metabolism</a> of living organisms that maintains the core temperature of the Earth and atmospheric conditions within a narrow self-regulating range of tolerance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lovelock03_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lovelock03-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Population_ecology">Population ecology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ecology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Population ecology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Population_ecology" title="Population ecology">Population ecology</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Lists_of_organisms_by_population" title="Lists of organisms by population">Lists of organisms by population</a></div>
<p>Population ecology studies the dynamics of species populations and how these populations interact with the wider environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Odum05_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Odum05-4">[4]</a></sup> A population consists of individuals of the same species that live, interact, and migrate through the same niche and habitat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waples06_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Waples06-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>A primary law of population ecology is the <a href="/wiki/Malthusian_growth_model" title="Malthusian growth model">Malthusian growth model</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turchin01_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Turchin01-49">[49]</a></sup> which states, "a population will grow (or decline) exponentially as long as the environment experienced by all individuals in the population remains constant."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turchin01_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Turchin01-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 18">: 18 </span></sup> Simplified population <a href="/wiki/Scientific_modelling" title="Scientific modelling">models</a> usually start with four variables: death, birth, <a href="/wiki/Immigration" title="Immigration">immigration</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Emigration" title="Emigration">emigration</a>.
</p><p>An example of an introductory population model describes a closed population, such as on an island, where immigration and emigration does not take place. Hypotheses are evaluated with reference to a null hypothesis which states that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Random" title="Random">random</a> processes create the observed data. In these island models, the rate of population change is described by:
</p>

</p><p>Population ecology builds upon these introductory models to further understand demographic processes in real study populations. Commonly used types of data include <a href="/wiki/Biological_life_cycle" title="Biological life cycle">life history</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fecundity" title="Fecundity">fecundity</a>, and survivorship, and these are analysed using mathematical techniques such as <a href="/wiki/Matrix_(mathematics)" title="Matrix (mathematics)">matrix algebra</a>. The information is used for managing wildlife stocks and setting harvest quotas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vandermeer03_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vandermeer03-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berryman92_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berryman92-51">[51]</a></sup> In cases where basic models are insufficient, ecologists may adopt different kinds of statistical methods, such as the <a href="/wiki/Akaike_information_criterion" title="Akaike information criterion">Akaike information criterion</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson00_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson00-52">[52]</a></sup> or use models that can become mathematically complex as "several competing hypotheses are simultaneously confronted with the data."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson04_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson04-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metapopulations_and_migration">Metapopulations and migration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ecology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Metapopulations and migration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Metapopulation" title="Metapopulation">Metapopulation</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Animal_migration" title="Animal migration">Animal migration</a></div>
<p>The concept of metapopulations was defined in 1969<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levins69_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levins69-54">[54]</a></sup> as "a population of populations which go extinct locally and recolonize".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levins70_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levins70-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 105">: 105 </span></sup> Metapopulation ecology is another statistical approach that is often used in <a href="/wiki/Conservation_biology" title="Conservation biology">conservation research</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith05_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith05-56">[56]</a></sup> Metapopulation models simplify the landscape into patches of varying levels of quality,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hanski98_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hanski98-57">[57]</a></sup> and metapopulations are linked by the migratory behaviours of organisms. Animal migration is set apart from other kinds of movement because it involves the seasonal departure and return of individuals from a habitat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nebel10_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nebel10-58">[58]</a></sup> Migration is also a population-level phenomenon, as with the migration routes followed by plants as they occupied northern post-glacial environments. Plant ecologists use pollen records that accumulate and stratify in wetlands to reconstruct the timing of plant migration and dispersal relative to historic and contemporary climates. These migration routes involved an expansion of the range as plant populations expanded from one area to another. There is a larger taxonomy of movement, such as commuting, foraging, territorial behaviour, stasis, and ranging. Dispersal is usually distinguished from migration because it involves the one-way permanent movement of individuals from their birth population into another population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clark98_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clark98-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dingle96_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dingle96-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>In metapopulation terminology, migrating individuals are classed as emigrants (when they leave a region) or immigrants (when they enter a region), and sites are classed either as sources or sinks. A site is a generic term that refers to places where ecologists sample populations, such as ponds or defined sampling areas in a forest. Source patches are productive sites that generate a seasonal supply of <a href="/wiki/Juvenile_(organism)" title="Juvenile (organism)">juveniles</a> that migrate to other patch locations. Sink patches are unproductive sites that only receive migrants; the population at the site will disappear unless rescued by an adjacent source patch or environmental conditions become more favourable. Metapopulation models examine patch dynamics over time to answer potential questions about spatial and demographic ecology. The ecology of metapopulations is a dynamic process of extinction and colonization. Small patches of lower quality (i.e., sinks) are maintained or rescued by a seasonal influx of new immigrants. A dynamic metapopulation structure evolves from year to year, where some patches are sinks in dry years and are sources when conditions are more favourable. Ecologists use a mixture of computer models and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Field_study" title="Field study">field studies</a> to explain metapopulation structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hanski04_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hanski04-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacKenzie06_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacKenzie06-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Community_ecology">Community ecology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ecology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Community ecology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Male_Lion_and_Cub_Chitwa_South_Africa_Luca_Galuzzi_2004_edit1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1333" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Male_Lion_and_Cub_Chitwa_South_Africa_Luca_Galuzzi_2004_edit1.jpg/220px-Male_Lion_and_Cub_Chitwa_South_Africa_Luca_Galuzzi_2004_edit1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Male_Lion_and_Cub_Chitwa_South_Africa_Luca_Galuzzi_2004_edit1.jpg/330px-Male_Lion_and_Cub_Chitwa_South_Africa_Luca_Galuzzi_2004_edit1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Male_Lion_and_Cub_Chitwa_South_Africa_Luca_Galuzzi_2004_edit1.jpg/440px-Male_Lion_and_Cub_Chitwa_South_Africa_Luca_Galuzzi_2004_edit1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Male_Lion_and_Cub_Chitwa_South_Africa_Luca_Galuzzi_2004_edit1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Interspecific interactions such as <a href="/wiki/Predation" title="Predation">predation</a> are a key aspect of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Community_ecology" title="Community ecology">community ecology</a>.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Community_ecology" title="Community ecology">Community ecology</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1062260506" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="quotebox pullquote floatleft" style="width:25%; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>Community ecology examines how interactions among species and their environment affect the abundance, distribution and diversity of species within communities.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">Johnson &amp; Stinchcomb (2007)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson07_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson07-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 250">: 250 </span></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>Community ecology is the study of the interactions among a collection of species that inhabit the same geographic area. Community ecologists study the determinants of patterns and processes for two or more interacting species. Research in community ecology might measure <a href="/wiki/Species_diversity" title="Species diversity">species diversity</a> in grasslands in relation to soil fertility. It might also include the analysis of predator-prey dynamics, competition among similar plant species, or mutualistic interactions between crabs and corals.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecosystem_ecology">Ecosystem ecology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ecology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Ecosystem ecology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_ecology" title="Ecosystem ecology">Ecosystem ecology</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1062260506" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="quotebox pullquote floatleft" style="width:25%; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>These ecosystems, as we may call them, are of the most various kinds and sizes. They form one category of the multitudinous physical systems of the universe, which range from the universe as a whole down to the atom.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">Tansley (1935)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tansley35_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tansley35-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 299">: 299 </span></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ecoecolfigure1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="372" data-file-width="569" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/44/Ecoecolfigure1.jpg/220px-Ecoecolfigure1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/44/Ecoecolfigure1.jpg/330px-Ecoecolfigure1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/44/Ecoecolfigure1.jpg/440px-Ecoecolfigure1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ecoecolfigure1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Riparian_forest" title="Riparian forest">riparian forest</a> in the <a href="/wiki/White_Mountains_(New_Hampshire)" title="White Mountains (New Hampshire)">White Mountains</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire" title="New Hampshire">New Hampshire</a> (USA) is an example of <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_ecology" title="Ecosystem ecology">ecosystem ecology</a></div></div></div>
<p>Ecosystems may be habitats within biomes that form an integrated whole and a dynamically responsive system having both physical and biological complexes. Ecosystem ecology is the science of determining the fluxes of materials (e.g. carbon, phosphorus) between different pools (e.g., tree biomass, soil organic material). Ecosystem ecologists attempt to determine the underlying causes of these fluxes. Research in ecosystem ecology might measure <a href="/wiki/Primary_production" title="Primary production">primary production</a> (g C/m^2) in a <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">wetland</a> in relation to decomposition and consumption rates (g C/m^2/y). This requires an understanding of the community connections between plants (i.e., primary producers) and the decomposers (e.g., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">fungi</a> and bacteria),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brinson81_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brinson81-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>The underlying concept of an ecosystem can be traced back to 1864 in the published work of <a href="/wiki/George_Perkins_Marsh" title="George Perkins Marsh">George Perkins Marsh</a> ("Man and Nature").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marsh64_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marsh64-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O'Neil01_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-O'Neil01-67">[67]</a></sup> Within an ecosystem, organisms are linked to the physical and biological components of their environment to which they are adapted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tansley35_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tansley35-64">[64]</a></sup> Ecosystems are complex adaptive systems where the interaction of life processes form self-organizing patterns across different scales of time and space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levin98_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levin98-68">[68]</a></sup> Ecosystems are broadly categorized as <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_ecosystem" title="Terrestrial ecosystem">terrestrial</a>, <a href="/wiki/Freshwater_ecosystem" title="Freshwater ecosystem">freshwater</a>, atmospheric, or <a href="/wiki/Marine_ecosystem" title="Marine ecosystem">marine</a>. Differences stem from the nature of the unique physical environments that shapes the biodiversity within each. A more recent addition to ecosystem ecology are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Technoecosystems" title="Technoecosystems">technoecosystems</a>, which are affected by or primarily the result of human activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Odum05_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-Odum05-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_webs">Food webs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ecology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Food webs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Food_web" title="Food web">Food web</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">Food chain</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:362px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chesapeake_Waterbird_Food_Web.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="440" data-file-width="565" decoding="async" height="280" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Chesapeake_Waterbird_Food_Web.jpg/360px-Chesapeake_Waterbird_Food_Web.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Chesapeake_Waterbird_Food_Web.jpg/540px-Chesapeake_Waterbird_Food_Web.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0e/Chesapeake_Waterbird_Food_Web.jpg 2x" width="360"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chesapeake_Waterbird_Food_Web.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Generalized food web of waterbirds from <a href="/wiki/Chesapeake_Bay" title="Chesapeake Bay">Chesapeake Bay</a></div></div></div>
<p>A food web is the archetypal <a href="/wiki/Ecological_network" title="Ecological network">ecological network</a>. Plants capture <a href="/wiki/Solar_energy" title="Solar energy">solar energy</a> and use it to synthesize <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Simple_sugars" title="Simple sugars">simple sugars</a> during <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>. As plants grow, they accumulate nutrients and are eaten by grazing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herbivores" title="Herbivores">herbivores</a>, and the energy is transferred through a chain of organisms by consumption. The simplified linear feeding pathways that move from a basal <a href="/wiki/Trophic_species" title="Trophic species">trophic species</a> to a top consumer is called the <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">food chain</a>. The larger interlocking pattern of food chains in an ecological community creates a complex food web. Food webs are a type of <a href="/wiki/Concept_map" title="Concept map">concept map</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Heuristic" title="Heuristic">heuristic</a> device that is used to illustrate and study pathways of energy and material flows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O'Neill86_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-O'Neill86-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pimm02_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pimm02-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pimm91_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pimm91-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>Food webs are often limited relative to the real world. Complete empirical measurements are generally restricted to a specific habitat, such as a cave or a pond, and principles gleaned from food web <a href="/wiki/Microcosm_(experimental_ecosystem)" title="Microcosm (experimental ecosystem)">microcosm</a> studies are extrapolated to larger systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Worm03_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Worm03-71">[71]</a></sup> Feeding relations require extensive investigations into the gut contents of organisms, which can be difficult to decipher, or stable isotopes can be used to trace the flow of nutrient diets and energy through a food web.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCann07_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-McCann07-72">[72]</a></sup> Despite these limitations, food webs remain a valuable tool in understanding community ecosystems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilbur97_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilbur97-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>Food webs exhibit principles of ecological emergence through the nature of trophic relationships: some species have many weak feeding links (e.g., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omnivores" title="Omnivores">omnivores</a>) while some are more specialized with fewer stronger feeding links (e.g., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predator" title="Predator">primary predators</a>). Theoretical and empirical studies identify <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Random#In_biology" title="Random">non-random</a> emergent patterns of few strong and many weak linkages that explain how ecological communities remain stable over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emmerson_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emmerson-74">[74]</a></sup> Food webs are composed of subgroups where members in a community are linked by strong interactions, and the weak interactions occur between these subgroups. This increases food web stability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kraus03_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kraus03-75">[75]</a></sup> Step by step lines or relations are drawn until a web of life is illustrated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pimm91_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pimm91-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Egerton07b_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Egerton07b-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shurin06_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shurin06-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards83_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards83-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trophic_levels">Trophic levels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ecology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Trophic levels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Trophic_level" title="Trophic level">Trophic level</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TrophicWeb.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1350" data-file-width="3260" decoding="async" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/TrophicWeb.jpg/450px-TrophicWeb.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/TrophicWeb.jpg/675px-TrophicWeb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/TrophicWeb.jpg/900px-TrophicWeb.jpg 2x" width="450"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TrophicWeb.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A trophic pyramid (a) and a food-web (b) illustrating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecological_relationship" title="Ecological relationship">ecological relationships</a> among creatures that are typical of a northern <a href="/wiki/Boreal_ecosystem" title="Boreal ecosystem">boreal</a> terrestrial ecosystem. The trophic pyramid roughly represents the biomass (usually measured as total dry-weight) at each level. Plants generally have the greatest biomass. Names of trophic categories are shown to the right of the pyramid. Some ecosystems, such as many wetlands, do not organize as a strict pyramid, because aquatic plants are not as productive as long-lived terrestrial plants such as trees. Ecological trophic pyramids are typically one of three kinds: 1) pyramid of numbers, 2) pyramid of biomass, or 3) pyramid of energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Odum05_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-Odum05-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 598">: 598 </span></sup></div></div></div>
<p>A trophic level (from Greek <i>troph</i>, τροφή, trophē, meaning "food" or "feeding") is "a group of organisms acquiring a considerable majority of its energy from the lower adjacent level (according to <a href="/wiki/Ecological_pyramid" title="Ecological pyramid">ecological pyramids</a>) nearer the abiotic source."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hariston93_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hariston93-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 383">: 383 </span></sup> Links in food webs primarily connect feeding relations or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trophism" title="Trophism">trophism</a> among species. Biodiversity within ecosystems can be organized into trophic pyramids, in which the vertical dimension represents feeding relations that become further removed from the base of the food chain up toward top predators, and the horizontal dimension represents the <a href="/wiki/Relative_species_abundance" title="Relative species abundance">abundance</a> or biomass at each level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Duffy07_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Duffy07-80">[80]</a></sup> When the relative abundance or biomass of each species is sorted into its respective trophic level, they naturally sort into a 'pyramid of numbers'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elton27_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elton27-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>Species are broadly categorized as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autotrophs" title="Autotrophs">autotrophs</a> (or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primary_producers" title="Primary producers">primary producers</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heterotrophs" title="Heterotrophs">heterotrophs</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Consumer_(food_chain)" title="Consumer (food chain)">consumers</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Detritivore" title="Detritivore">Detritivores</a> (or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decomposers" title="Decomposers">decomposers</a>). Autotrophs are organisms that produce their own food (production is greater than respiration) by photosynthesis or <a href="/wiki/Chemosynthesis" title="Chemosynthesis">chemosynthesis</a>. Heterotrophs are organisms that must feed on others for nourishment and energy (respiration exceeds production).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Odum05_4-5"><a href="#cite_note-Odum05-4">[4]</a></sup> Heterotrophs can be further sub-divided into different functional groups, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primary_consumers" title="Primary consumers">primary consumers</a> (strict herbivores), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trophic_dynamics" title="Trophic dynamics">secondary consumers</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carnivorous" title="Carnivorous">carnivorous</a> predators that feed exclusively on herbivores), and tertiary consumers (predators that feed on a mix of herbivores and predators).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-David03_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-David03-82">[82]</a></sup> Omnivores do not fit neatly into a functional category because they eat both plant and animal tissues. It has been suggested that omnivores have a greater functional influence as predators because compared to herbivores, they are relatively inefficient at grazing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oksanen91_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oksanen91-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>Trophic levels are part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holistic" title="Holistic">holistic</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Complex_systems" title="Complex systems">complex systems</a> view of ecosystems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Loehle88_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Loehle88-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ulanowicz79_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ulanowicz79-85">[85]</a></sup> Each trophic level contains unrelated species that are grouped together because they share common ecological functions, giving a macroscopic view of the system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li00_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Li00-86">[86]</a></sup> While the notion of trophic levels provides insight into energy flow and top-down control within food webs, it is troubled by the prevalence of omnivory in real ecosystems. This has led some ecologists to "reiterate that the notion that species clearly aggregate into discrete, homogeneous trophic levels is fiction."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polis96_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Polis96-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 815">: 815 </span></sup> Nonetheless, recent studies have shown that real trophic levels do exist, but "above the herbivore trophic level, food webs are better characterized as a tangled web of omnivores."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson07_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson07-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 612">: 612 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Keystone_species">Keystone species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ecology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Keystone species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Keystone_species" title="Keystone species">Keystone species</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sea_otters_holding_hands,_cropped.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="418" data-file-width="978" decoding="async" height="94" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Sea_otters_holding_hands%2C_cropped.jpg/220px-Sea_otters_holding_hands%2C_cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Sea_otters_holding_hands%2C_cropped.jpg/330px-Sea_otters_holding_hands%2C_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Sea_otters_holding_hands%2C_cropped.jpg/440px-Sea_otters_holding_hands%2C_cropped.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sea_otters_holding_hands,_cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sea_otter" title="Sea otter">Sea otters</a>, an example of a keystone species</div></div></div>
<p>A keystone species is a species that is connected to a disproportionately large number of other species in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food-web" title="Food-web">food-web</a>. Keystone species have lower levels of biomass in the trophic pyramid relative to the importance of their role. The many connections that a keystone species holds means that it maintains the organization and structure of entire communities. The loss of a keystone species results in a range of dramatic cascading effects that alters trophic dynamics, other food web connections, and can cause the extinction of other species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fisher06_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fisher06-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Libralato06_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Libralato06-90">[90]</a></sup>. The term keystone species was coined by Robert Paine in 1969 and is a reference to the <a href="/wiki/Keystone_(architecture)" title="Keystone (architecture)">keystone</a> architectural feature as the removal of a keystone species can result in a community collapse just as the removal of the keystone in an arch can result in the arch's loss of stability <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sea_otter" title="Sea otter">Sea otters</a> (<i>Enhydra lutris</i>) are commonly cited as an example of a keystone species because they limit the density of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_urchins" title="Sea urchins">sea urchins</a> that feed on <a href="/wiki/Kelp" title="Kelp">kelp</a>. If sea otters are removed from the system, the urchins graze until the kelp beds disappear, and this has a dramatic effect on community structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mills93_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mills93-92">[92]</a></sup> Hunting of sea otters, for example, is thought to have led indirectly to the extinction of the <a href="/wiki/Steller%27s_sea_cow" title="Steller's sea cow">Steller's sea cow</a> (<i>Hydrodamalis gigas</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson95_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson95-93">[93]</a></sup> While the keystone species concept has been used extensively as a <a href="/wiki/Conservation_biology" title="Conservation biology">conservation</a> tool, it has been criticized for being poorly defined from an operational stance. It is difficult to experimentally determine what species may hold a keystone role in each ecosystem. Furthermore, food web theory suggests that keystone species may not be common, so it is unclear how generally the keystone species model can be applied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mills93_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mills93-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polis00_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Polis00-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Complexity">Complexity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ecology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Complexity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Complexity" title="Complexity">Complexity</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Emergence" title="Emergence">Emergence</a></div>
<p>Complexity is understood as a large computational effort needed to piece together numerous interacting parts exceeding the iterative memory capacity of the human mind. Global patterns of biological diversity are complex. This <a href="/wiki/Biocomplexity" title="Biocomplexity">biocomplexity</a> stems from the interplay among ecological processes that operate and influence patterns at different scales that grade into each other, such as transitional areas or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecotones" title="Ecotones">ecotones</a> spanning landscapes. Complexity stems from the interplay among levels of biological organization as energy, and matter is integrated into larger units that superimpose onto the smaller parts. "What were wholes on one level become parts on a higher one."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Novikoff45_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Novikoff45-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 209">: 209 </span></sup> Small scale patterns do not necessarily explain large scale phenomena, otherwise captured in the expression (coined by Aristotle) 'the sum is greater than the parts'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schneider01_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schneider01-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Molnar04_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Molnar04-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="ref_E_1"><a href="#cnote_E">[E]</a></sup>
